   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d303638331" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Antimony（Sb） has high theoretical specific capacity and moderate reaction potential, but poor cycling stability and rate performance caused by large volume expansion and sluggish reaction kinetics limit its development in alkali-ion batteries. The construction of carbon/Sb composites can solve this problem, but the traditional single carbon composite and poor Sb/carbon interface have limited promotion. Here, under the guidance of theoretical calculations, a strategy of hierarchical double carbon composite is proposed to enhance the performance of Sb anode, and systematically reveal the lithium/sodium ions (Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup>) storage mechanism of Sb in complex composite system. Sb nanoparticles (Sb NPs) are encapsulated in carbon sphere matrix with strong interfacial chemical bond to form the first level composite material, and then the highly conductive and mechanically strong graphene is used as a three-dimensional skeleton network to connect the first level composite to form the second level composite material. The hierarchical double carbon/Sb composite exhibits excellent rate (228 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> @ 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and long cycling performance (373.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> @ 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 2200 cycles, capacity retention of 93.1%). This work may expand structural design and interface regulation of Sb/C composites and providing theoretical guidance for metal anodes development.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202311478-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202311478-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The use of antimony (Sb) as anode for lithium/sodium ion batteries has attracted wide attention from researchers due to its high theoretical specific capacity (660&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and moderate reaction voltage (0.3–0.8&nbsp;V vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li or Na<sup>+</sup>/Na).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0001_R_d303638323e367" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - J. Power Sources">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0004_R_d303638323e367" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, as a typical alloy-type negative electrode material, Sb-based electrodes undergo severe volume expansion during lithiation or sodiation process, which damages the electrode structure, causes material pulverization, detachment from the current collector, and leads to a sharp deterioration in battery performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0005_R_d303638323e373" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>–</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0009_R_d303638323e376" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nanomaterials">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To address the issue of volume expansion in Sb-based materials, researchers have conducted extensive studies, primarily focusing on two aspects: designing Sb nanostructures with different morphologies and introducing carbon component.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0010_R_d303638323e383" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Lett.">10</a>-<a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0012_R_d303638323e383" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Nano">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The rational structure and morphology can provide sufficient buffer space for volume expansion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0013_R_d303638323e388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Mater. Lett.">13</a>-<a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0015_R_d303638323e388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Carbon">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, nanostructures can shorten the diffusion paths for ions and electrons, accelerate the reaction kinetics, and enhance the rate performance of the battery. The use of carbon materials for encapsulation and cross-linking is also a viable approach. For example, designing carbon-encapsulated Sb nanodots,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0016_R_d303638323e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Small Methods">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sb/carbon eggshell-like composites,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0017_R_d303638323e399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Lett.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> carbon-coated Sb nanorod,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0018_R_d303638323e404" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nanoscale">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc. The introduction of carbon provides protection for antimony, offering expansion buffer space while preventing material pulverization and loss, thus improving long cycling performance. Carbon materials can also increase the contact area between the material and electrolyte, enhance wetting speed and degree, accelerate reaction kinetics, and improve the electrochemical performance of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0019_R_d303638323e410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">19</a>-<a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0026_R_d303638323e410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Rare Met.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Since 2004, graphene has been considered a new type of material and is also known as the cornerstone of carbon materials. Graphene oxide (GO) is an important branch of graphene research, obtained by dispersing graphene through graphite oxidation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0027_R_d303638323e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Nano">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> GO possesses abundant carboxyl, hydroxyl, and epoxy groups on its edges and surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0028_R_d303638323e422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These oxygen functional groups confer various physicochemical properties, including electronic, optical, thermal, mechanical, electrochemical properties, and chemical reactivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0029_R_d303638323e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Rev.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> GO and its derivatives, such as reduced graphene oxide (rGO), exhibit diverse structures and excellent properties, finding wide applications in electrochemical energy storage, including secondary batteries, capacitors, fuel cells, and more.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0030_R_d303638323e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Energy Chem.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Remarkably, conductive rGO serves to expedite electron transfer, enhance electronic conductivity, and adeptly absorb strain during cycling within alkali metal ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0031_R_d303638323e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Eng. J.">31</a>-<a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0033_R_d303638323e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Chem. Asian J.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Based on the discussions above, in this work, sodium antimony gluconate (C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>37</sub>Na<sub>3</sub>O<sub>27</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub>) was used as a precursor to prepare spherical composite materials of carbon-shell-encapsulated Sb nanodots through high-temperature calcination. Furthermore, GO was employed as a structural regulator to achieve the encapsulation and connection of C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>37</sub>Na<sub>3</sub>O<sub>27</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub> by simple mixing and calcination, resulting in Sb-C composites with 3D graphene frameworks attached to carbon spheres. The introduction of rGO sheets can increase the specific surface area of the composite material, effectively prevent the agglomeration of carbon sphere, significantly improve the rate performance, and enhance long cycling stability under high current density. When used as an electrode material for lithium ion batteries (LIBs), spherical particles derived from C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>37</sub>Na<sub>3</sub>O<sub>27</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub> and GO (SS-GO) delivers a discharge specific capacity of 299&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even at a high current density of 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and retains a reversible specific capacity of 373.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention rate of 93.1% at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 2200 cycles. As the anode for sodium ion batteries (SIBs), it exhibits a discharge specific capacity of 355&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 3&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and shows 350&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 120 cycles at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202311478-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202311478-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202311478-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202311478-sec-0030-title">2.1 Theoretical Calculations</h3>
            
            <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations are commonly employed in the exploration of the affinity and reaction between Li and Sb, as well as in the investigation of the atomic-level interactions between oxygen-containing functional groups and the carbon matrix. In this study, specific DFT models of Sb and Sb─O─C are constructed, as illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0001">1a,c</a>, and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. These models enable a detailed examination of the electronic structure and bonding characteristics, providing valuable insights into the fundamental processes occurring at the interface between Li, Sb, and the carbon matrix. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0001">1b,d</a> depicts the Li<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion pathways of Sb and Sb─O─C, respectively, showcasing their distinct migration patterns. Furthermore, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0001">1e</a> displays the corresponding diffusion energy barriers for Sb and Sb-O-C as the migration path undergoes constant changes. It is worth noting that the energy barrier for Sb is measured to be 1.37&nbsp;eV, approximately four times higher than that of Sb-O-C (0.33&nbsp;eV). This significant disparity in energy barriers suggests a reduced diffusion resistance for Li<sup>+</sup> ions across the heterointerface of Sb─O─C, thereby enhancing the overall ion conductivity. These findings highlight the advantageous role of the Sb─O─C heterointerface in facilitating the efficient transport of Li<sup>+</sup> ions within the material system. Furthermore, the density of states (DOS) calculated for the Sb/C complex incorporating the Sb─O─C bond reveals a narrow band gap of ≈0.074&nbsp;eV in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0001">1f</a>. This observation suggests that the composite retains excellent conductivity and exhibits high electron transport efficiency even after the introduction of the heterointerface formed by the conductive carbon matrix. The narrow band gap further supports the notion of enhanced electron mobility within the composite material, underscoring its potential for efficient charge transport.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202311478-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3b645985-5159-4d90-b309-ccf1570001ca/adfm202311478-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3b645985-5159-4d90-b309-ccf1570001ca/adfm202311478-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ee8b2ea8-349d-4cc6-b709-bb66cb689eb0/adfm202311478-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3b645985-5159-4d90-b309-ccf1570001ca/adfm202311478-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202311478-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202311478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural configurations of a) Sb and c) Sb-O-C in DFT calculations. Li<sup>+</sup> migration pathway of b) Sb and d) Sb─O─C. (e) Comparison of Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barrier between Sb and Sb─O─C. f) Density of states profiles of Sb─O─C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202311478-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202311478-sec-0040-title">2.2 Structural Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>Theoretical findings demonstrate the beneficial influence of constructing Sb─O─C interfacial bonds on the transport of lithium ions and electrons. Motivated by these results, spherical particles derived from C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>37</sub>Na<sub>3</sub>O<sub>27</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub> (SS) and SS-GO were fabricated through the pyrolysis of C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>37</sub>Na<sub>3</sub>O<sub>27</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub> with Sb─O─C in the molecular structure, and the synthesis schematic is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0002">2</a>. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a–</a> (Supporting Information), the precursor displays spherical morphology with uneven size and rough surface, and severe agglomeration behavior can be observed. As seen in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2e</a> (Supporting Information), the elements in the precursor are evenly distributed, and there is no localized enrichment of elements.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202311478-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/34d80959-76bb-4694-92e0-5aef7c9126a2/adfm202311478-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/34d80959-76bb-4694-92e0-5aef7c9126a2/adfm202311478-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/366f70de-25c2-4744-97aa-8899db79b843/adfm202311478-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/34d80959-76bb-4694-92e0-5aef7c9126a2/adfm202311478-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202311478-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202311478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram of the synthesis of SS and SS-GO.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To investigate the optimal reaction temperature, a series of reaction temperatures ranging from 200 to 600&nbsp;°C were selected for calcination, and the phase composition was tested after cooling, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). At lower temperatures, the precursor exhibited an amorphous state without any discernible diffraction peaks. The resulting product appeared brown, indicating incomplete carbonization and the absence of a reduction reaction. However, at a reaction temperature of 280&nbsp;°C, the reduction reaction was initiated, as evidenced by the presence of diffraction peaks corresponding to metallic antimony (PDF#35-0732) in the product. Nevertheless, the material exhibited poor crystallinity at this temperature. As the temperature was further increased, the crystallinity of the product improved, accompanied by increased diffraction peak intensity and enhanced clarity.</p>
            
            <p>The morphology of the product SS after high-temperature calcination differs significantly from that of the precursor, as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0003">3</a>. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0003">3a,b</a>, SS consists of densely distributed spheres with irregular size, and some small spheres interconnected to form large voluminous spherical aggregates. The surface of SS is smoother compared to the precursor. And the gaps between the spheres are able to alleviate material expansion during volume changes. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0003">3c</a> provides a visual observation of embedded black particles sparsely distributed within the material through the transmission electron microscopy (TEM) result. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0003">3d</a>, elliptical Sb nanoparticles (Sb NPs) with sizes ranging from 10 to 30&nbsp;nm are embedded in amorphous carbon, resembling raisins embedded in bread. This carbon-coated structure with embedded Sb NPs can provide buffer space for volume expansion and prevent the fragmentation and loss of Sb, thereby enhancing the long cycling performance of the material. The high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0003">3e</a>) displays a circular region of lattice fringes of Sb NPs within SS, surrounded by amorphous carbon. The lattice fringe spacing of 0.32&nbsp;nm corresponds to the (012) crystal plane of elemental Sb (PDF#35-0732). Furthermore, in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0003">3f</a>, the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern exhibits two distinct diffraction rings corresponding to the (003) and (104) crystal planes. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0003">3g</a> reveals the uniform distribution of C and O elements, while Sb element appears locally enriched, indicating the formation of an outer shell containing C and O after high-temperature carbonization of C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>37</sub>Na<sub>3</sub>O<sub>27</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub>, with Sb being reduced and enriched as embedded nanoparticles within the carbon layer.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202311478-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/97e1f767-47e9-4b7d-9848-eb65d84f1188/adfm202311478-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/97e1f767-47e9-4b7d-9848-eb65d84f1188/adfm202311478-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/64624eb9-2a26-4c0e-b601-6a59519752e7/adfm202311478-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/97e1f767-47e9-4b7d-9848-eb65d84f1188/adfm202311478-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202311478-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202311478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) SEM, c,d) TEM, e) HRTEM, f) SAED, and g) corresponding element mapping images of SS composite.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>After introducing GO that had been reduced to rGO in the H<sub>2</sub>/Ar atmosphere, a noticeable change in the morphology of the SS-GO product is observed in scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0004">4a,b</a>. The sheet-like rGO forms an ultra-thin coating on the surface of Sb/C composite spheres, connecting composite spheres of varying sizes, it is observed that some smaller composite spheres adhere to the surface of rGO. This interconnection between rGO and composite spheres can effectively increase the material's specific surface area, facilitating contact and infiltration with the electrolyte, thus accelerating reaction kinetics. Furthermore, the cross-supported 3D structure increases the material's porosity, providing sufficient space for expansion and buffering, which predicts a certain advantage of this structure in energy storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0034_R_d303638323e693" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Energy Storage Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0004">4c,d</a>, compared to the SS product, the arrangement of Sb NPs in SS-GO becomes denser and more uniformly distributed. The Sb NPs exhibit a tendency toward a more circular shape, with sizes ranging from ≈10 to 20&nbsp;nm. Additionally, the carbon shell of the composite spheres in SS-GO is thinner and tightly integrated with the external layer of rGO, forming a “nested extension” dual-layer carbon structure. This configuration can enhance the material's mechanical properties to a certain extent, thereby improving the stability of the electrode material. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0004">4e,g</a> exhibits lattice fringes of 0.318 and 0.190&nbsp;nm, respectively, corresponding to the (012) and (006) crystal planes of metal Sb (PDF#35-0732), which is consistent with the X-ray diffraction (XRD) test results. In addition to the lattice fringes of Sb, in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0004">4g,h</a> reveal the lattice fringes of graphene (0.319&nbsp;nm), demonstrating the interconnection between rGO and the originally amorphous carbon derived from pyrolyzing C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>37</sub>Na<sub>3</sub>O<sub>27</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub>, even directly contacting the lattice fringes of Sb. This hetero-interface gives rise to intrinsic electric fields which can accelerate the transport of ions and electrons, enhancing reaction kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0035_R_d303638323e719" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nano Energy">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Multiple diffraction rings are observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0004">4f</a>, indicating that the SS-GO material also possesses a polycrystalline structure. Among them, two distinct diffraction rings correspond to the (003) and (104) crystal planes. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0004">4i</a>, compared to SS, the distribution of Sb elements in SS-GO is more concentrated, which is attributed to the high density of Sb NPs, consistent with TEM results. Furthermore, the uniform distribution of C and O between the composite spheres confirm the connecting effect of rGO.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202311478-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0d1bf8f0-ab20-4320-bba7-24737d291550/adfm202311478-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0d1bf8f0-ab20-4320-bba7-24737d291550/adfm202311478-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dfb46877-d532-4406-a9db-15afe42a3669/adfm202311478-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0d1bf8f0-ab20-4320-bba7-24737d291550/adfm202311478-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202311478-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202311478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) SEM, c,d) TEM, e,g,h) HRTEM, f) SAED and i) corresponding element mapping images of SS-GO composite.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) depicts the morphological and structural characterization results of commercial antimony powder (bulk Sb). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a,b</a> (Supporting Information) illustrates the morphology of bulk Sb at different magnifications, revealing an overall irregular block-like shape with a rough surface, where adjacent blocks are directly stacked. TEM results in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4c,d</a> (Supporting Information) confirm that the bulk Sb consists of solid blocks. The HRTEM image in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4e</a> (Supporting Information) exhibits continuous, clear, and parallel lattice fringes. Notably, the lattice spacing of 0.314&nbsp;nm corresponds to the (012) crystal plane of pure Sb (PDF#35-0732). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4f</a> (Supporting information) displays the SAED pattern, showcasing regularly arranged diffraction spots, indicating that the bulk Sb is a single-crystal material. These diffraction spots correspond to the (104) and (003) crystal planes, indicating a high level of purity. Consequently, by utilizing bulk Sb as a reference sample, direct comparisons can be made between the electrochemical performance differences of bare Sb and Sb/C composites.</p>
            
            <p>A comprehensive set of characterization techniques was employed to analyze the physicochemical properties of the three electrode materials. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0005">5a</a> depicts the XRD spectra of the products SS-GO, SS, and bulk Sb. The diffraction peaks of SS and SS-GO matched those of the standard card (PDF#35-0732), indicating that the Sb element in the product exists in the form of metal antimony after carbon thermal reduction. The pore size distribution and specific surface area of the three materials were determined by nitrogen adsorption/desorption isotherms. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0005">5b</a> reveals that both the SS and SS-GO samples exhibit type IV adsorption isotherms and distinct H3 hysteresis loops.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0036_R_d303638323e779" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Power Sources">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The specific surface areas are measured to be 24.80 and 30.28 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for SS and SS-GO, respectively, which are significantly higher than the bulk Sb's value of 0.35&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This result further confirms that rGO forms a thin coating on the surface of the spherical structures in SS-GO and extends outward, contributes to the increased specific surface area. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0005">5c</a>, the SS-GO sample exhibits a hierarchical porous structure, mainly consisting of mesopores below 50&nbsp;nm and macropores above 100&nbsp;nm, and 20&nbsp;nm mesopores dominate. Generally, the presence of mesopores can increase the active sites for Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption, thereby promoting pseudocapacitive energy storage behavior.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0037_R_d303638323e801" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Energy Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The macropores can not only provide buffering space but also ensure the infiltration and permeation of the electrolyte, accelerating ion transport rates and enhancing reaction kinetics. In comparison to the SS-GO composite, the SS material has a single-pore structure, primarily composed of mesopores in the range of 0–50&nbsp;nm, and with fewer mesopores overall. The bulk Sb exhibits almost no pore structure, which is consistent with the results from SEM and TEM images.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202311478-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ab5606ea-350b-4931-8c8c-30a5b8f4dd82/adfm202311478-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ab5606ea-350b-4931-8c8c-30a5b8f4dd82/adfm202311478-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9480bb1a-f7c8-46f4-9199-595b2f69889a/adfm202311478-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ab5606ea-350b-4931-8c8c-30a5b8f4dd82/adfm202311478-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202311478-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202311478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns, b) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption isothermal curves, c) the pore size distribution curves, d) Raman spectra of SS, SS-GO and bulk Sb. e)TG curves and f)FT-IR spectra of SS, SS-GO. XPS patterns of SS, SS-GO and bulk Sb. g) XPS full spectrum, high-resolution XPS spectra of h) C 1s, i) Sb 3d and O 1s.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The Raman spectra of the three materials are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0005">5d</a>. Due to the presence of a carbon matrix in SS and SS-GO, two broad peaks appear at 1340 and 1590&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, representing the degree of defects (D-band) and graphitic structure (G-band), respectively. The I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> is 0.92 for SS and 0.87 for SS-GO, indicating a higher graphitization degree for the latter, which is consistent with the abundant graphene lattice detected in the TEM images. Furthermore, two sharp peaks appear at 113 and 152&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to the E<sub>g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> bands of Sb. In comparison to the two composite materials, the peaks in the bulk Sb are sharper, which may be attributed to the carbon coating on the Sb NPs in the composites, resulting in fewer exposed surfaces and weaker peak intensity.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The thermogravimetric (TG) curves of SS and SS-GO are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0005">5e</a>, allowing for the quantitative determination of Sb content in the composites. The mass change is mainly attributed to the combustion of carbon materials in air and the oxidation of elemental Sb to Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> at high temperatures, corresponding to the following reaction equations:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202311478-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/01f83f7a-317d-4dde-bed1-0789bffbadd1/adfm202311478-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202311478:adfm202311478-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202311478-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                              <mi>Sb</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mo>→</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>Sb</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                                 <mn>4</mn>
                              </msub>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}2{\mathrm{Sb}} + 2{{\mathrm{O}}}_2 \to {\mathrm{Sb}}_2{{\mathrm{O}}}_4\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202311478-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/19ef2eec-fce8-4782-b894-b5d68d756c09/adfm202311478-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202311478:adfm202311478-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202311478-math-0002.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mo>→</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>CO</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msub>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{C}} + {{\mathrm{O}}}_2 \to {\mathrm{CO}}_2\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Based on the above reaction processes, assuming the mass change is denoted as w, the general formula for calculating the Sb content can be derived as follows:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202311478-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/aa0791a3-8ace-4113-a890-6ef8c60d9e05/adfm202311478-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202311478:adfm202311478-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202311478-math-0003.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>Sb</mi>
                                 <mo>%</mo>
                                 <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                 <mo linebreak="goodbreak">=</mo>
                                 <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              </mrow>
                              <mfrac>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                       <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                       <mo>×</mo>
                                       <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    </mrow>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
                                       <mi>Sb</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <msub>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>Sb</mi>
                                          <mn>2</mn>
                                       </msub>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi>
                                          <mn>4</mn>
                                       </msub>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </msub>
                              </mfrac>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                 <mo linebreak="badbreak">×</mo>
                                 <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                 <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
                                 <mn>100</mn>
                                 <mo>%</mo>
                                 <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                 <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                                 <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">w</mi>
                                 <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
                              </mrow>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{Sb\% \ = \ }}\frac{{{\mathrm{2\ \times \ }}{{\mathrm{M}}}_{{\mathrm{Sb}}}}}{{{{\mathrm{M}}}_{{\mathrm{Sb}}_{\mathrm{2}}{{\mathrm{O}}}_{\mathrm{4}}}}}{\mathrm{\ \times \ (100\% \ -\ w)}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>Here, M represents the relative molecular mass. According to the above formula, the calculated Sb contents in SS and SS-GO are 66.17% and 64.66%, respectively. These values indicate a relatively high Sb content in the composites. The appropriate mass ratio not only ensures the uniform distribution of Sb and C, reducing volumetric expansion stress, but also avoids excessive sacrifice of the energy density of the active material.</p>
            
            <p>Fourier-transform infrared (FTIR) spectrometer was used to determine the types of surface functional groups in the materials, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0005">5f</a>. Absorption peaks were observed at ≈1627, 1374, and 1080&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to ─C═C, ─CH<sub>3</sub> and ─C─O, respectively. This indicates the presence of multiple functional groups in the carbon matrix of the composite material. Furthermore, a prominent absorption peak was detected at 605&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, providing further evidence of the formation of Sb─O─C bonds. The presence of these interface chemical bonds can influence the electronic interactions at the heterogeneous interface of the composite material, enhancing interface coupling, accelerating electron/ion transport, and thereby improving the reaction kinetics.</p>
            
            <p>X-ray Photoelectron Spectroscopy (XPS) was employed to investigate the surface chemical states of the materials. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0005">5g</a> displays the overall elemental spectrum, revealing signals from Sb, O, and C elements. The weak C signal detected in the commercial antimony powder may be attributed to interference from CO<sub>2</sub> in the air. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0005">5h,i</a> presents the detailed spectra of the C element and Sb/O elements, respectively. Both SS and SS-GO exhibit five characteristic peaks in the C element spectrum, and their positions are close to each other. Taking SS as an example, the peaks located at 284.63, 285.91, 286.83, 287.80, and 289.77&nbsp;eV correspond to the vibrations of C─C/C═C, C─O, C─O─C, C═O, and ─CO<sub>x</sub> bonds, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0038_R_d303638323e987" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Nano">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This indicates that C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>37</sub>Na<sub>3</sub>O<sub>27</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub> underwent carbon thermal reduction, resulting in the generation of a significant number of oxygen-containing functional groups. These groups are beneficial for the formation of interface chemical bonds and the construction of a stable Sb-C heterogeneous interface. The Sb 3d high-resolution XPS spectrum of SS-GO exhibits two pairs of double peaks. One pair is located at 530.68 and 540.09&nbsp;eV, corresponding to the vibrations of Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and 3d<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0039_R_d303638323e1008" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Small">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The other pair is situated at 528.44 and 537.88&nbsp;eV, indicating the presence of Sb<sub>0</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0016_R_d303638323e1016" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Small Methods">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, two characteristic peaks related to O are detected at 531.30 and 533.24&nbsp;eV, corresponding to the vibrations of Sb-O/Sb-O─C and C─O/C═O/─OH, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0040_R_d303638323e1021" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nano Energy">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>The oxygen-containing characteristic peaks in the bulk Sb are a result of surface oxidation during storage and testing. In comparison to the bulk Sb, the Sb 3d characteristic peaks in SS and SS-GO exhibit a shift toward lower binding energies. This could be attributed to the enhanced interface coupling between the carbon matrix and Sb, which influences the electronic structure of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0016_R_d303638323e1026" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Small Methods">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202311478-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202311478-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Performance in LIBs</h3>
            
            <p>To comprehensively evaluate the electrochemical performance differences among the composite materials SS, SS-GO, and bulk Sb, galvanostatic charge–discharge tests were conducted on the three samples within a voltage window of 0.01–2.5&nbsp;V.</p>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0006">6a</a> presents the rate performance test results of the three electrode materials at different current densities. During 8 cycles at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 3.0, 4.0, and 5.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the average discharge specific capacities of the SS-GO electrode were found to be 677, 538, 472, 429, 394, 369, 350, and 333&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively while SS electrode exhibited average discharge specific capacities of 578, 425, 364, 325, 285, 260, 242, and 229&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. At an equivalent current density, the SS-GO electrode consistently demonstrates a discharge specific capacity ≈100&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> higher than the SS electrode. However, the bulk Sb, used as a reference sample, showed inferior rate performance, with average specific capacities of 648, 503, 407, 264, 152, 123, 96, and 74&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at corresponding current densities. It is noteworthy that a sharp drop in specific capacity was observed for the commercial material at a current density of 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating its poor ability to rapidly store lithium at high currents. Moreover, when the current density was reduced from 5.0 to 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific discharge capacity of SS-GO reached 560&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and remained stable. SS exhibited a discharge specific capacity of 490&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, with a slight decrease in subsequent cycles. In contrast, the discharge specific capacity of the bulk Sb was 408&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which directly dropped to 206&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the following 8 charge–discharge cycles. These results highlighted that the introduction of double-layer carbon coating structure not only significantly improved the rate performance, but also ensured the structure stability of the electrode material, facilitating the adaptation to fluctuations in current.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202311478-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a976f2ee-0598-437d-8d90-ce9f6dbac6ae/adfm202311478-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a976f2ee-0598-437d-8d90-ce9f6dbac6ae/adfm202311478-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dc1f9ebe-d74d-4202-b1ea-8932898b498e/adfm202311478-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a976f2ee-0598-437d-8d90-ce9f6dbac6ae/adfm202311478-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202311478-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202311478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The lithium-ions storage capabilities: a) Rate capability and b) the corresponding discharge/charge profiles at various rates of SS-GO, SS and bulk Sb. c) High-rate capability and d) cycling performance of SS, SS-GO and bulk Sb at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and e) corresponding discharge/charge profiles at various cycles of SS-GO. Cycling performances of SS, SS-GO and bulk Sb anodes at f) 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and (g) 4&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The charge–discharge curves in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0006">6b</a> also confirm these findings. For the bulk Sb, when the current density increased to 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the charge–discharge plateau drastically shortened, indicating an incomplete alloying process of Sb at this stage, which severely affected the charge–discharge capacity of the material. At the initial low current, the alloying reaction process of the SS-GO electrode exhibited longer voltage plateaus compared to the SS electrode. This could be attributed to the smaller and more uniform Sb NPs in the former, providing more reactive sites and promoting a more thorough reaction. The double-layer carbon structure demonstrated remarkable stability, enabling it to adapt to the rapid charge–discharge process at high currents. As the current density continued to increase, the length of the alloying reaction plateau gradually decreased and remained relatively flat.</p>
            
            <p>Given the excellent rate performance of SS-GO, further evaluation of its fast-charging capability was conducted by increasing the current density continuously, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0006">6c</a>. The reversible specific capacities at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, and 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> were 690, 581, 488, 437, 399, 350, and 299&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Even when tested at an ultra-high current density of 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the SS-GO electrode still exhibited a high reversible specific capacity of 228&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. It is worth noting that the SS electrode also demonstrated considerable reversible specific capacity at this current density. However, a significant difference in reversible specific capacity was observed between the two when the current was restored to 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The SS-GO electrode maintained a reversible specific capacity of 483&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, nearly identical to the previous reversible specific capacity, indicating the structural integrity and remarkable stability of the SS-GO electrode even after multiple cycles at high current densities. These results highlight the substantial potential of SS-GO as a high-rate lithium storage material.</p>
            
            <p>The cycling performance is a crucial indicator for evaluating the electrochemical performance of electrode materials. Therefore, long-term cycling tests were conducted on the three electrode materials at different current densities. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0006">6d</a> shows the cycling performance at a low current density of 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, where the initial coulombic efficiency of SS-GO was 68%, while SS exhibited 64%. By comparing the charge–discharge curves, it can be observed that the initial discharge specific capacities of the two electrode materials were similar, but SS had a significantly lower initial charge specific capacity. This reduction in specific capacity was attributed to the shortened reaction plateau, indicating a higher occurrence of side reactions during the alloying process of SS. After 100 charge–discharge cycles, the reversible specific capacity of SS-GO reached 576.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, higher than the 472.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> achieved by SS. However, the bulk Sb electrode showed a noticeable capacity decay after 10 cycles, ultimately exhibiting a reversible specific capacity of only 130.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The charge–discharge curves for the 1st, 3rd, 16th, 35th, 56th, and 100th cycles were plotted, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0006">6e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). The alloying reaction plateau of the bulk Sb electrode continuously shortened, indicating irreversible charge–discharge reactions caused by structural collapse during the cycling process. As for the SS-GO and SS electrodes, their charge–discharge curves showed a high degree of overlap after the first three cycles, and there were no significant changes in the alloying reaction plateau, indicating a relatively stable structure of the electrode material.</p>
            
            <p>Three electrodes were cycled 2200 times at a high current density of 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and the results are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0006">6f</a>. Among them, SS-GO demonstrated excellent cycling stability, maintaining a reversible specific capacity of 373.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 93.1%, which was significantly higher than that of SS (169.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and bulk Sb (24.4 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) under the same conditions. When the current density was increased to 4&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the long-term cycling stability of the three electrode materials was evaluated after 2200 cycles, as shown in Figure 6g.SS-GO still exhibited a remarkably high reversible specific capacity and excellent cycling performance, with a discharge specific capacity of 326.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and a capacity retention of 81.8%. However, the reversible specific capacity of the SS electrode continuously decreased after 600 cycles, eventually reaching 135.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. It is worth noting that these three electrode materials with different morphologies showed their own trends in capacity variation. SS-GO experienced a slight attenuation in reversible specific capacity, while the specific capacity of the SS electrode remained stable during the first 100 cycles, then increased until around 450 cycles and remained relatively stable before starting to decline after 100 cycles. On the other hand, the bulk Sb electrode exhibited a sharp decline in specific capacity.</p>
            
            <p>To investigate the reasons behind these cycling differences, the electrode cycled at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> current density for 450 and 1600 cycles were characterized for morphology and structural changes to study the microscopic variations of the active materials during charge and discharge processes. The SEM images visually demonstrate the morphological changes of the three electrode materials before and after cycling, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0007">7</a>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0007">7,a</a>1–a3 corresponds to sequential scans of electrodes fabricated from bulk Sb, representing their pre-cycling state, 450 cycles and 1600 cycles at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Initially, the bulk Sb is irregular. After 450 cycles, the structural integrity of the active material is disrupted, and severe agglomeration is observed, with the surface even showing numerous indentations from glass fibers. At this point, the structure of the active material is destroyed, rendering it nearly incapable of providing effective capacity. Similar observations are obtained after 1600 cycles. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0007">7,b</a>1–b3 displays SEM images of SS electrode before cycling, after 450 cycles and 1600 cycles at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Prior to any charge–discharge cycling, the composite material on the electrode film consists of large-volume spherical aggregates surrounded by conductive carbon, with some small-volume carbon-coated Sb NPs exposed. After 450 cycles, the morphology of the composite material is well-preserved, but the surface of the spherical particles changes from smooth to rough, accompanied by the appearance of cracks. This condition, without collapse of the sphere structure, facilitates the exposure and activation of more active sites, increases the contact between the Sb NPs inside the spheres and the electrolyte, thereby enhancing the charge–discharge specific capacity of the material. This observation aligns with the continuous increase in reversible specific capacity observed during the initial charge–discharge cycles. As the reaction progresses, the rupture of carbon spheres leads to insufficient mechanical support, causing overall structural damage to the material and resulting in a sustained decline in reversible specific capacity. After 1600 cycles, the surface of the SS electrode film exhibits agglomeration, transforming into irregular solid blocks, accompanied by a lower reversible specific capacity. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0007">7,c</a>1–c3 shows the three distinct states of SS-GO electrode under pre-cycling, after 450 and 1600 cycles at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. It can be observed that prior to cycling, the electrode film contains rGO sheets. In comparison to the smooth exposed surface of carbon-encapsulated Sb NPs in SS, the carbon spheres in SS-GO are coated with a thin layer of graphene oxide, and even trace amounts of conductive carbon black are attached. This configuration enhances the conductivity of the active material. After 450 cycles, the structural integrity of the active material is well-preserved, with the presence of clearly visible rGO on the surface and no cracks in the carbon-antimony spheres. The rGO that connects the spheres also remains relatively intact. These observations are consistent with the excellent cycling performance exhibited by the composite material. After 1600 charge–discharge cycles, the individual presence of rGO and Sb-C spheres on the electrode surface is no longer distinguishable. However, they still maintain an independent and clearly defined block-like state, with some areas even showing spherical solid structures under high-resolution SEM images. Additionally, there is no evidence of the sheet-like agglomeration observed in the previous two electrode materials. At this stage, the electrode material has reached a stable state.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202311478-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/80cf9007-5c6f-4f3d-9888-ced798556aa3/adfm202311478-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/80cf9007-5c6f-4f3d-9888-ced798556aa3/adfm202311478-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/28c176a7-7d7e-4532-ac08-6ae98cfe222f/adfm202311478-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/80cf9007-5c6f-4f3d-9888-ced798556aa3/adfm202311478-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202311478-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202311478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of bulk Sb, SS and SS-GO electrodes in LIBs with different stages: (a1–c1) before cycling, (a2–c2) after 450 cycles and (a3–c3) after 1600 cycles at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>SEM images were employed to observe the surface morphology changes of the materials, primarily focusing on the alterations in the shape of carbon spheres and rGO during the reaction process. On the other hand, TEM was utilized to investigate the changes in the Sb NPs encapsulated within the carbon spheres. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0008">8,a</a>1–a3,b1–b3 depict the TEM images of the SS electrode after undergoing 450 and 1600 charge–discharge cycles, respectively. In contrast to the pristine state of the composite material, the size of Sb NPs decreases after 450 cycles, reaching ≈2–6&nbsp;nm, and their shapes transform from elliptical to nearly circular morphology, exhibiting a uniform distribution within the carbon substrate. The lattice fringes with a spacing of 0.303&nbsp;nm correspond to the (012) crystal plane of metal Sb (PDF#35-0732). This change provides a plausible explanation for the capacity increase of SS anode in the early 400 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0006">6f</a>). As the size of Sb NPs reduce, new active sites become exposed, leading to an increased number of sodium storage sites and a shortened ion migration path. Consequently, the capacity experiences a certain degree of improvement. After 1600 cycles, the morphology of the Sb NPs becomes blurred. This can be attributed to the significant volume expansion of the nanoparticles during alloying, causing their fragmentation and subsequent partial recombination upon repeated charge–discharge cycles. As a result, larger elliptical-shaped Sb NPs with non-uniform distribution emerge. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0008">8,c</a>1–c3,d1–d3 depict TEM images of the SS-GO electrode after 450 and 1600 charge–discharge cycles, respectively. Similar to the SS electrode, the Sb NPs on the SS-GO exhibit a decreasing trend in size, with a more concentrated size range of ≈3–4&nbsp;nm. However, individual nanoparticles appear fuzzy with unclear edges, which can be attributed to the dense distribution of Sb NPs within the material. This dense distribution does not provide sufficient space to accommodate the volume expansion resulting from antimony alloying, aligning with the slight decrease in capacity observed during the initial cycles. The lattice fringes with a spacing of 0.209&nbsp;nm correspond to the (110) crystal plane of metal Sb (PDF#35-0732). After 1600 cycles, the fuzziness of the Sb NPs intensifies, but it remains superior to that observed in the SS electrode under the same conditions. Moreover, HRTEM analysis reveals minimal changes in the size of the Sb NPs, ≈4–6&nbsp;nm in diameter. The lattice fringes with a spacing of 0.304&nbsp;nm correspond to the (012) crystal plane of metal Sb (PDF#35-0732). It is noteworthy that after 450 and 1600 cycles, individual Sb NPs adopt a nearly circular shape, particularly after 1600 cycles, providing a distinct contrast to the Sb NPs in the SS electrode.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202311478-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/917a1bac-8169-4753-9819-ee4ce66cd0d2/adfm202311478-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/917a1bac-8169-4753-9819-ee4ce66cd0d2/adfm202311478-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a79484ba-50c9-4006-b2e2-be36003048db/adfm202311478-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/917a1bac-8169-4753-9819-ee4ce66cd0d2/adfm202311478-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202311478-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202311478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM and HRTEM images of SS electrode in LIBs after 450 cycles (a1–a3) and 1600 cycles (b1–b3) at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. TEM and HRTEM images of SS-GO electrode in LIBs after 450 cycles (c1–c3) and 1600 cycles (d1–d3) at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In summary, SS spheres can provide structural support and stable reversible specific capacity within a short-term cycling period (first 100 cycles). The thick carbon coating hinders the infiltration and penetration of the electrolyte, resulting in a lower initial specific capacity. As the reaction progresses, cracks appear in the carbon-antimony spheres, increasing the specific surface area and providing more reactive sites, leading to a continuous increase in specific capacity. After multiple cycles in this state, the spherical structure of the material is damaged, resulting in a continuous decrease in specific capacity. The SS-GO electrode features a dense distribution of Sb NPs internally, surrounded by an ultrathin carbon layer. This configuration facilitates sufficient contact between the active material and the electrolyte, thereby providing a higher reversible specific capacity during the initial charge–discharge stage. The introduction of rGO can enhance the mechanical performance of the material and partially suppress the fragmentation and aggregation of Sb─C spheres, ensuring the integrity and stability of the structure. Additionally, during the cycling process, the deformation and loss of Sb NPs within the SS electrode are more severe compared to the SS-GO electrode. The distribution of Sb NPs within the carbon layer is also more uneven in the SS electrode, which leads to the lower specific capacity of SS compared to SS-GO.</p>
            
            <p>Cycling tests were conducted at a constant current density of 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and the charge–discharge curves for the first three cycles were plotted.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0041_R_d303638323e1261" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Mater. Chem. Front.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The results are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0009">9a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a,b</a> (Supporting Information). The alloying reaction plateau in the discharge curve of the first cycle is slightly lower than the plateaus in the second and third cycles. Furthermore, around 0.75&nbsp;V, both SS and SS-GO electrodes exhibit the connection of two similar plateaus in their initial discharge curves, representing the stepwise process of Sb alloying. Compared to the SS electrode, the SS-GO electrode shows a higher degree of overlap in the charge–discharge curves, indicating better reversibility. Additionally, from the charge–discharge curves, it can be observed that antimony exhibits lower charge–discharge voltages, longer and more stable plateaus, making it a highly promising anode.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202311478-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a26f6258-170c-4dec-ad2b-a0268f5c2575/adfm202311478-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a26f6258-170c-4dec-ad2b-a0268f5c2575/adfm202311478-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/74ef7858-aa03-4c5c-9ba4-e575439220e8/adfm202311478-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a26f6258-170c-4dec-ad2b-a0268f5c2575/adfm202311478-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202311478-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202311478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Lithium storage mechanism: a) Initial three charge–discharge curves at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and b) initial three CV curves of SS-GO electrodes at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. c) In situ XRD contour plots with the corresponding charge/discharge curve and the phase transformation. d) Schematic diagram of the reaction.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To investigate the lithium storage mechanism of the composite products, cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were performed at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The results are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0009">9b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6c,d</a> (Supporting Information). During the initial cathodic scan, the bulk Sb exhibited an extremely sharp reduction peak at 0.65&nbsp;V, indicating the first entry of Li<sup>+</sup> into the material and alloying reaction with Sb, accompanied by some irreversible side reactions. An intense peak was also observed during the oxidation process at 1.1&nbsp;V, corresponding to the lithium extraction process. The subsequent two scan cycles did not overlap with the first cycle, which can be attributed to the activation of the material and the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film during the initial lithiation process.</p>
            
            <p>In-situ XRD testing technique was employed to investigate the phase transition process and lithium storage mechanism of SS-GO within the voltage range of 0.01–2.5&nbsp;V. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0009">9c</a> shows the contour plot of the diffraction peak intensities in the 20°−30°, 37°−39.5°, and 39.5°−43° diffraction angle ranges, where the color becomes redder as the intensity increases. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information) presents the corresponding XRD patterns. On the right side, the constant current charge–discharge curves and the phase changes during the reaction process are displayed. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0009">9d</a> displays schematic diagram of the reaction inside SS-GO. The strong diffraction peaks at 38.7° and 41.4° originate from the BeO substrate, which was used as the current collector in the in-situ XRD testing process. Initially, prominent diffraction peaks corresponding to the (012), (104), and (110) crystal planes of Sb are observed. As the discharge reaction progresses, these three characteristic peaks gradually weaken, indicating the gradual penetration of Li<sup>+</sup> into the crystal lattice of Sb and the formation of the alloying product Li<sub>3</sub>Sb (PDF#04-0791). The increasing intensity of the diffraction peaks corresponding to the (111), (200), and (220) crystal planes at 23.2°, 27.4°, and 38.9°, respectively, suggests the important stage of alloying reaction. During the charge process, the signal of the intermediate product Li<sub>3</sub>Sb gradually diminishes, while the peaks of Sb reappear and gradually strengthen. The characteristic peaks of Sb exhibit no shift from complete disappearance to reappearance, indicating the thoroughness of the reaction and the excellent reversibility of the material. Based on the above analysis, the reaction equation corresponding to the lithium storage process of the SS-GO composite electrode is as follows:</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">(I)alloying reaction

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202311478-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/88c3681c-90d2-4ece-92f2-32586c896316/adfm202311478-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202311478:adfm202311478-math-0004" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202311478-math-0004.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>Li</mi>
                                 <mo>+</mo>
                              </msup>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">+</mo>
                              <mi>Sb</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>
                                 <mo>−</mo>
                              </msup>
                              <mo>→</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>Li</mi>
                                 <mn>3</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mi>Sb</mi>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}3{\mathrm{Li}}^ + + {\mathrm{Sb}} + 3{{\mathrm{e}}}^ - \to {\mathrm{Li}}_3{\mathrm{Sb}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">(II)de-alloying reaction

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202311478-disp-0005"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f08c45a0-091f-4265-8894-60e501d753db/adfm202311478-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202311478:adfm202311478-math-0005" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202311478-math-0005.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>Li</mi>
                                 <mn>3</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mi>Sb</mi>
                              <mo>→</mo>
                              <mi>Sb</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>Li</mi>
                                 <mo>+</mo>
                              </msup>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>
                                 <mo>−</mo>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{Li}}_3{\mathrm{Sb}} \to {\mathrm{Sb}} + 3{\mathrm{Li}}^ + + 3{{\mathrm{e}}}^ - \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(5)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>By combining charge–discharge curves, CV curves, and in situ XRD testing techniques, we comprehensively investigate the alloying/dealloying reaction process of elemental Sb and provide an atomic model of the phase transformation. This step reaction represents the terminal process of various Sb-based materials in electrochemical reactions. A clear understanding of the reaction mechanism is of great significance for analyzing different types of antimony-based materials.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The kinetics properties play a crucial role in determining the rate performance and cycling stability of electrodes. The charge storage mechanisms of different electrodes were evaluated through CV curves at different scan rates. CV curves were recorded at scan rates of 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0010">10a</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a,b</a> (Supporting Information). It is noteworthy that as the scan rate increases to 0.6&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, the reduction peak representing the alloying process in the bulk Sb gradually weakens, and the shape of the CV curve exhibits irregular variations. This is attributed to the structural degradation of the electrode material and the collapse of Li<sup>+</sup> delithiation pathways at higher scan rates, indicating poor cyclic reversibility of the bulk Sb. As for the SS and SS-GO electrodes, the CV curves show regular enlargement with increasing scan rates. The reduction/oxidation peak positions of the SS-GO electrode exhibit smaller shifts, indicating lower polarization and superior reversibility and stability. These results are consistent with the electrochemical performance test results.

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202311478-disp-0006"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8d6d0e6e-0c1b-48e3-93f2-fc6fc4476d82/adfm202311478-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202311478:adfm202311478-math-0006" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202311478-math-0006.png">
                        <semantics>

                              <mi>i</mi>
