               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202311478-disp-0007"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202311478-fig-0010"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fa0dbf73-aa0e-45ae-9f9a-5e34586e76c8/adfm202311478-fig-0010-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/fa0dbf73-aa0e-45ae-9f9a-5e34586e76c8/adfm202311478-fig-0010-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fc584b44-61e9-49fe-b1fe-0e97baffea41/adfm202311478-fig-0010-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fa0dbf73-aa0e-45ae-9f9a-5e34586e76c8/adfm202311478-fig-0010-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 10<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202311478-fig-0010&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202311478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The kinetic analysis of LIBs: a) CV curves of SS-GO anode at 0.2–1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. (b) The liner relationship of log (<i>i</i>) versus log (<i>v</i>) of SS-GO anode. The liner relationship between <i>v</i><sup>0.5</sup> and <i>Ip</i> of c) R1 and d) O1 with bulk Sb, SS and SS-GO anodes. e) Capacitive-controlled contributions of SS and SS-GO anodes at different scan rates. f) GITT profiles and the relationship between discharge g) and charge h) voltages and the diffusion coefficients of Li<sup>+</sup> of SS, SS-GO and bulk Sb anodes. i) EIS curves of SS, SS-GO and bulk Sb anodes before cycling. j) EIS curves of SS and SS-GO anodes after 450 cycles at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Based on Equations&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202311478-disp-0006" title="Link to equation">6</a>) and (<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202311478-disp-0007" title="Link to equation">7</a>), the rate-controlling steps and capacitance contribution steps during the lithium storage process can be explored. Here, <i>i</i> represents the current, <i>v</i> represents the scan rate, and a and b are constants. The fitting results of the b values are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0010">10b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c,d</a> (Supporting Information), where the bulk Sb exhibits diffusion control. In contrast, the b values for SS and SS-GO are both within the range of 0.5–1, indicating a mixed mechanism for charge storage in both materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0038_R_d303638323e1577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Nano">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The pseudocapacitive behavior of the SS-GO electrode is significantly higher than that of the SS electrode. The Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Li+</sub>) during the charge and discharge process can be calculated using Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202311478-disp-0008" title="Link to equation">8</a>). Comparing the slopes allows us to determine the rate of Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion, where a steeper slope indicates faster diffusion, while a shallower slope indicates slower diffusion. From Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0010">10c,d</a>, it can be observed that the Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion rate in SS-GO material is higher than that in SS material, and both are higher than that in bulk Sb. The CV curves can be used to quantitatively calculate the pseudocapacitive contribution at specific scan rates. In Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202311478-disp-0009" title="Link to equation">9</a>), k<sub>1</sub><i>v</i> represents the pseudocapacitive-controlled part, and k<sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sub>1/2</sub> represents the diffusion-controlled part. The results are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0010">10e</a>. Both SS and SS-GO exhibit higher pseudocapacitive contribution rates, indicating that the stable Sb─O─C interface bonds contribute to improved reaction kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0042_R_d303638323e1611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Carbon">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, as the scan rate increases, the pseudocapacitive contribution percentage of the material increases, and the surface redox reactions accelerate. When the scan rate is 1.0&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, the pseudocapacitive contribution percentages for SS and SS-GO are 78.24% and 79.25%, respectively.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) can also be used to calculate the diffusion rate of Li<sup>+</sup> ions. The equation is as follows:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202311478-disp-0010"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/1253418d-b3b2-4294-a8db-6ca33e5f655a/adfm202311478-math-0010.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202311478:adfm202311478-math-0010" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202311478-math-0010.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mfrac>
                                 <mn>4</mn>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>π</mi>
                                    <mi>τ</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mfrac>
                              <msup>
                                 <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <msub>
                                          <mi>mV</mi>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
                                       </msub>
                                       <mi>MS</mi>
                                    </mfrac>
                                 </mfenced>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msup>
                              <msup>
                                 <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">E</mi>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">E</mi>
                                          <mi>τ</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                 </mfenced>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{D}} = \frac{4}{{\pi \tau }}{\left( {\frac{{{\mathrm{mV}}_{\mathrm{M}}}}{{{\mathrm{MS}}}}} \right)}^2{\left( {\frac{{\Delta {{\mathrm{E}}}_{\mathrm{S}}}}{{\Delta {\mathrm{E}}\tau }}} \right)}^2\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(10)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>The variable <i>m</i> represents the mass of the active material, while <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> and <i>M</i> represent the molar volume and molar mass of the active material, respectively. <i>S</i> represents the electrode area, and Δ<i>E</i><sub>S</sub> and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> represent the steady-state voltage drop caused by the current pulse and the voltage drop during the constant current pulse, respectively. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0010">10f</a> shows the “time-voltage” response curve of the samples. It is noteworthy that both SS and SS-GO exhibit similar diffusion trends, indicating similar ion diffusion behavior. The dynamic relationship between the apparent chemical diffusion coefficient of Li<sup>+</sup> in the final samples and the voltage can be observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0010">10g,h</a>. Both SS and SS-GO demonstrate higher Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion rates, surpassing those of bulk Sb by one to two orders of magnitude. This suggests that these two composite materials possess superior reaction kinetics, potentially due to the formation of stable heterointerfaces and Sb-O-C interface bonds between the carbon-based shell and the internal antimony nanodots, which effectively facilitate the rapid ion transport.</p>
            
            <p>The charge transport and ion diffusion rates of the materials were evaluated using electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) testing. According to the fitting results in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0010">10i</a>, the R<sub>ct</sub> values of the three electrode materials are relatively similar before cycling. The R<sub>ct</sub> value of the bulk Sb is only 70.87&nbsp;Ω, which can be attributed to the excellent electronic conductivity of metallic Sb. The thin carbon shell of SS-GO shortens the transport path and the ultra-thin graphene oxide layer on the surface increases the contact between the electrolyte and the electrode material, forming fast transport channels and conductive networks. As a result, SS-GO exhibits superior electronic conductivity with a smaller R<sub>ct</sub> value. In the case of the SS electrode, the thicker carbon layer surrounding the Sb NPs may result in longer and less accessible transport paths, which hinders charge conduction. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0010">10j</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a> (Supporting Information) show the EIS spectra after 450 cycles at a current density of 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Both SS and SS-GO electrodes exhibit very low charge transfer resistances, measuring 8.39 and 8.25&nbsp;Ω, respectively. After the cycling, the materials undergo sufficient activation, exposing more active sites and demonstrating higher conductivity. In particular, the SS electrode shows a significant change in R<sub>ct</sub> value before and after cycling, which is closely related to the continuous activation process during cycling and consistent with the capacity increase observed in the initial stages of long cycling. On the other hand, the bulk Sb suffers severe structural damage after cycling, resulting in almost complete electrochemical performance failure and an elevated Rct value of 2300&nbsp;Ω. For SS and SS-GO electrodes, EIS testing after an additional 1150 cycles at a current density of 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> reveals that the R<sub>ct</sub> value of SS-GO remains close to 9.5&nbsp;Ω, similar to the value obtained after 450 cycles. The result in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a> (Supporting Information) is the same. These indicate the excellent stability of the material. All fitting results are shown in <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adfm202311478-tbl-0001">1</a>.</p>

                           <td class="center-aligned">23.6</td>
                           </tr>
                        </tbody>
                     </table>
               </div>
               
               
               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202311478-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202311478-sec-0060-title">2.4 Electrochemical Performances in SIBs</h3>
            
            <p>Due to the outstanding electrochemical performance of SS-GO electrode material in lithium-ion batteries, it has been tested as an anode for sodium-ion batteries. First, rate performance tests were conducted, and the results were consistent with the performance observed in SIBs, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0011">11a</a>. At a current density of 3&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, it still exhibited a discharge specific capacity of 355&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and the specific capacity remained stable as the current density increased to 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The charge–discharge curves at different current densities, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0011">11b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a,b</a> (Supporting Information), also confirm this point effectively. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0011">11c</a> presents the cyclic performance of the three electrodes at a current density of 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, where it can be observed that the SS-GO electrode experiences almost no capacity decay after 100 cycles and still provides a specific capacity of 400&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0011">11d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10c,d</a> (Supporting Information) demonstrate that SS-GO exhibits better energy storage capabilities, with a longer reaction platform and superior stability, which are crucial for maintaining high capacity. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0011">11e,f</a> illustrates the cyclic performance of the three electrode materials. It is evident from the figures that even after 150 cycles at a current density of 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, SS-GO still delivers a discharge specific capacity of 385&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and after 120 cycles at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, it provides a discharge specific capacity of 350&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202311478-fig-0011"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a081c11a-4d4d-4349-80e4-7c11a5fa0ece/adfm202311478-fig-0011-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a081c11a-4d4d-4349-80e4-7c11a5fa0ece/adfm202311478-fig-0011-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/34dae8d3-75e3-41ae-a7a5-3f64662d31f4/adfm202311478-fig-0011-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a081c11a-4d4d-4349-80e4-7c11a5fa0ece/adfm202311478-fig-0011-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 11<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202311478-fig-0011&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202311478" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The sodium-ions storage capabilities and sodium storage mechanism: a) Rate capability and b) the corresponding discharge/charge profiles at various rate of SS-GO. c) Cycling performance of SS, SS-GO and bulk Sb at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and d) corresponding discharge/charge profiles at various cycles of SS-GO. Cycling performances of SS, SS-GO and bulk Sb anodes at e) 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and f) 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. g) Initial three CV curves of SS-GO electrodes at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. h) In situ XRD contour plots with the corresponding charge/discharge curve and the phase transformation.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The sodium storage mechanism of SS-GO was comprehensively investigated by using CV curves and in situ XRD analysis. The experimental results depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0011">11g</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a,b</a> (Supporting Information) provide valuable insights into the sequential stages of the sodium storage process in Sb metal, which occur at distinct potentials of 0.75, 0.45, and 0.22&nbsp;V (R1, R2, and R3), respectively. These three reduction peaks correspond to the successive binding of Sb with 3 Na<sup>+</sup>. To gain further understanding, the phase evolution of the products during the charge–discharge cycling was probed in detail, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0011">11h</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information). Notably, during the initial discharge phase, the characteristic diffraction peaks associated with the prominent crystallographic planes (003), (012), (110), and (104)) of metallic Sb exhibit a gradual decline in intensity. This process is also accompanied by the formation of amorphous Na<sub>x</sub>Sb, which cannot be captured by in situ XRD easily. Remarkably, upon reaching a voltage of ≈0.5&nbsp;V, a discernible transformation occurs, manifested by the emergence of distinct diffraction peaks at approximate angles of 21.4°, 33.5°, 34.0°, and 38.8°. These new peaks unequivocally mark the completion of the Na<sub>3</sub>Sb alloying process, corroborating the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into the Sb lattice structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0043_R_d303638323e2064" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Energy Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, an evident oxidation peak (O2) initially appears at ≈0.85&nbsp;V in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202311478-fig-0011">11g</a>, corresponding to the de-alloying process of Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. In subsequent scans, as the material-interface stabilizes gradually, another oxidation peak (O1) emerges at a lower potential, further substantiating that the de-alloying process is stepwise. This phenomenon is also observed in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a and S14</a> (Supporting Information). Taking the analysis above into consideration, the reaction equation corresponding to the sodium storage process of the SS-GO composite electrode can be summarized as follows:</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">(I)alloying reaction

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202311478-disp-0011"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e2aea581-0736-4c6d-a7c9-38c566a66dcf/adfm202311478-math-0011.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202311478:adfm202311478-math-0011" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202311478-math-0011.png">

                                 <mi>Na</mi>

                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(11)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">(II)de-alloying reaction

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202311478-disp-0012"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/fc59dcc6-c8e4-4ee6-bfa3-9ab67b565f90/adfm202311478-math-0012.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202311478:adfm202311478-math-0012" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202311478-math-0012.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>Na</mi>
                                 <mn>3</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mi>Sb</mi>
                              <mo>→</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>Na</mi>
                                 <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mn>3</mn>
                                       <mo>−</mo>
                                       <mi>x</mi>
                                    </mrow>
                                 </mfenced>
                              </msub>
                              <mi>Sb</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <mi>x</mi>

                                 <mo>−</mo>
                              </msup>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>

                              <msup>
                                 <mi>Na</mi>
                                 <mo>+</mo>
                              </msup>
                              <mo>→</mo>
                              <mi>Sb</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <mn>3</mn>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>Na</mi>
                                 <mo>+</mo>
                              </msup>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <mn>3</mn>

                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>
                                 <mo>−</mo>
                              </msup>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mn>0</mn>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">&lt;</mo>
                                    <mi>x</mi>
                                    <mo>&lt;</mo>
                                    <mn>3</mn>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mfenced>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{Na}}_3{\mathrm{Sb}} \to {\mathrm{Na}}_{\left( {3 - x} \right)}{\mathrm{Sb}} + x{{\mathrm{e}}}^ - + x{\mathrm{Na}}^ + \to {\mathrm{Sb}} + 3{\mathrm{Na}}^ + + 3{{\mathrm{e}}}^ - \left( {0 &amp;lt; x &amp;lt; 3} \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(12)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>It's worth noting that both the alloying reactions of Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> with Sb are stepwise processes. However, due to the difference in ionic radii between Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>, their binding rates differ, resulting in variations in the number of oxidation and reduction peaks. The stepwise binding process is more evident from the CV curves of the sodium storage process. Additionally, the number and intensity of the peaks are also influenced by the material's intrinsic properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0044_R_d303638323e2220" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Mater. Chem. A">44</a>-<a href="#adfm202311478-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202311478-bib-0048_R_d303638323e2220" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Mater. Chem. A">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>CV measurements were systematically conducted across a range of scan rates (0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> to gain insights into the electrochemical performance of the investigated materials, as exemplified in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a and S14a,b</a> (Supporting Information). The obtained CV curves manifest distinct reduction peaks during the scanning process, followed by two discernible oxidation peaks. Notably, the CV profiles of SS-GO material exhibit remarkable enhancements and display well-defined redox features as the scan rate escalates, providing compelling evidence for the superior stability of SS-GO over pristine SS and bulk Sb counterparts.</p>
            
            <p>The analysis of fitting results pertaining to the b values, presented in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13b and S15a,b</a> (Supporting Information), reveals that bulk Sb predominantly exhibits diffusion-controlled behavior. In contrast, both SS and SS-GO materials exhibit b values falling within the range of 0.5–1, signifying a mixed charge storage mechanism. Strikingly, the SS-GO electrode showcases significantly augmented pseudocapacitive behavior compared to the SS electrode. As portrayed in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13c and S16</a> (Supporting Information), the diffusion rate of Na<sup>+</sup> within the SS-GO material surpasses that of the SS material, and both outperform the bulk Sb counterpart. Additionally, both SS and SS-GO exhibit elevated pseudocapacitive contribution rates, as illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information), which further highlights the beneficial role of stable Sb─O─C interface bonds in facilitating improved reaction kinetic. Importantly, at a scan rate of 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the pseudocapacitive contribution percentages for SS and SS-GO reach noteworthy values of 82% and 86%, respectively.</p>
            
            <p>Furthermore, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13d</a> (Supporting Information) uncovers analogous diffusion trends for both SS and SS-GO, implying comparable ion diffusion behaviors between these materials. The dynamic correlation between the apparent chemical diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the final samples and the voltage is effectively visualized in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13e,f</a> (Supporting Information). Notably, both SS and SS-GO materials exhibit heightened Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion rates, underscoring their favorable transport properties. The fitting outcomes of EIS, as depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13g,h</a> (Supporting Information), further reinforce the exceptional stability of the SS-GO material. Comprehensive details of the fitting results can be found in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Combined with all above results, the SS-GO anode exhibits splendid electrochemical performance, which is better than that of many previously reported Sb-based anode materials for LIBs and SIBs, as listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202311478-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202311478-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, aiming at the key problems of poor structural stability of Sb anode materials and slow ion transport at the interface of Sb/carbon composite materials, based on theoretical calculation, the synergetic modification strategy of dual-carbon confine and Sb─O─C interfacial chemical bond was proposed. Sb NPs encapsulated in carbon spheres with Sb─O─C interfacial chemical bond was constructed by pyrolyzing C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>37</sub>Na<sub>3</sub>O<sub>27</sub>Sb<sub>2</sub>, and rGO was in-situ introduced to connect and coat the primary Sb/C composite to form hierarchical double carbon modified Sb composites (SS-GO). Combined with mechanism analysis, it can be demonstrated that the ultra-thin external carbon layer, strong interfacial chemical bond, and robust hierarchical structure of the SS-GO composite provided excellent accessibility for electrolyte infiltration, shortening the ion/electron transport pathways and enhancing the kinetic rates. Therefore, the SS-GO composite material not only exhibited excellent rate performance, demonstrating high practical specific capacity while adapting to rapid changes in current density, but also showcases exceptional long-term cycling performance. When used as anode for LIBs, it delivers a discharge specific capacity of 299&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even at a high current density of 10&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, showcasing outstanding fast charging capability. Furthermore, after 2200 cycles at a high current density of 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, it still retains a reversible specific capacity of 373.7 &nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, with a capacity retention rate of 93.1%. When employed as anode for SIBs, it exhibits a discharge specific capacity of 355&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 3&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and after 120 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, it provides a discharge specific capacity of 350&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This work deviates from the conventional approaches of single carbon-coated Sb with poor Sb/C interface, it expands the design of Sb/C composite materials and opens new avenues for interface modulation.</p>
         </section>
